Achilles\' heel was the only part of his body which was not invulnerable to damage by human weaponry. Before they could take Troy, the Greeks had to steal from the citadel the wooden image of Pallas Athena (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palladium_(mythology)" title="Palladium (mythology)">Palladium</a>). Finally, with Athena\'s help, they built the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_Horse" title="Trojan Horse">Trojan Horse</a>. Despite the warnings of Priam\'s daughter <a href="/wiki/Cassandra" title="Cassandra">Cassandra</a>, the Trojans were persuaded by <a href="/wiki/Sinon" title="Sinon">Sinon</a>, a Greek who feigned desertion, to take the horse inside the walls of Troy as an offering to Athena; the priest Laocoon, who tried to have the horse destroyed, was killed by sea-serpents. At night the Greek fleet returned, and the Greeks from the horse opened the gates of Troy. In the total sack that followed, Priam and his remaining sons were slaughtered; the Trojan women passed into slavery in various cities of Greece. The adventurous homeward voyages of the Greek leaders (including the wanderings of <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">Odysseus</a> and Aeneas (the <i>Aeneid</i>), and the murder of Agamemnon) were told in two epics, the Returns (the lost <i><a href="/wiki/Nostoi" title="Nostoi">Nostoi</a></i>) and Homer\'s <i>Odyssey</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeliosTr_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeliosTr-69">[69]</a></sup> The Trojan cycle also includes the adventures of the children of the Trojan generation (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orestes_(mythology)" title="Orestes (mythology)">Orestes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telemachus" title="Telemachus">Telemachus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrBr_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-TrBr-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Trojan War provided a variety of themes and became a main source of inspiration for Ancient Greek artists (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metope_(architecture)" title="Metope (architecture)">metopes</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a> depicting the sack of Troy); this artistic preference for themes deriving from the Trojan Cycle indicates its importance to the Ancient Greek civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeliosTr_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-HeliosTr-69">[69]</a></sup> The same mythological cycle also inspired a series of posterior European literary writings. For instance, Trojan Medieval European writers, unacquainted with Homer at first hand, found in the Troy legend a rich source of heroic and romantic storytelling and a convenient framework into which to fit their own courtly and chivalric ideals. Twelfth-century authors, such as <a href="/wiki/Beno%C3%AEt_de_Sainte-Maure" title="Beno&#238;t de Sainte-Maure">Beno&#238;t de Sainte-Maure</a> (<i>Roman de Troie</i> [Romance of Troy, 1154&#8211;60]) and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_of_Exeter" title="Joseph of Exeter">Joseph of Exeter</a> (<i>De Bello Troiano</i> [On the Trojan War, 1183]) describe the war while rewriting the standard version they found in <i>Dictys</i> and <i>Dares</i>. They thus follow <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>\'s advice and Virgil\'s example: they rewrite a poem of Troy instead of telling something completely new.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the more famous heroes noted for their inclusion in the Trojan War were:\n</p><p><i>On the Trojan side:</i>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Aeneas</li>\n<li>Hector</li>\n<li>Paris</li></ul>\n<p><i>On the Greek side:</i>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Ajax (there were two Ajaxes)</li>\n<li>Achilles</li>\n<li>King Agamemnon</li>\n<li>Menelaus</li>\n<li>Odysseus</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_and_Roman_conceptions_of_myth">Greek and Roman conceptions of myth</span></h2>\n<p>Mythology was at the heart of everyday life in Ancient Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson15_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson15-71">[71]</a></sup> Greeks regarded mythology as a part of their history. They used myth to explain natural phenomena, cultural variations, traditional enmities and friendships. It was a source of pride to be able to trace the descent of one\'s leaders from a mythological hero or a god. Few ever doubted that there was truth behind the account of the Trojan War in the <i>Iliad</i> and <i>Odyssey</i>. According to <a href="/wiki/Victor_Davis_Hanson" title="Victor Davis Hanson">Victor Davis Hanson</a>, a military historian, columnist, political essayist and former <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a> professor, and John Heath, a classics professor, the profound knowledge of the Homeric <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epos</a> was deemed by the Greeks the basis of their acculturation. Homer was the "education of Greece" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#7961;&#955;&#955;&#940;&#948;&#959;&#962; &#960;&#945;&#943;&#948;&#949;&#965;&#963;&#953;&#962;</span>), and his poetry "the Book".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanson37_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanson37-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_myth">Philosophy and myth</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plato-raphael.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="262" data-file-width="246" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Plato-raphael.jpg/220px-Plato-raphael.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Plato-raphael.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plato-raphael.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>\'s Plato in <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i> fresco (probably in the likeness of <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>). The philosopher expelled the study of Homer, of the tragedies and of the related mythological traditions from his utopian <i>Republic</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the rise of philosophy, history, prose and <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> in the late 5th century&#160;BC, the fate of myth became uncertain, and mythological genealogies gave place to a conception of history which tried to exclude the supernatural (such as the <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydidean</a> history).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffin80_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin80-73">[73]</a></sup> While poets and dramatists were reworking the myths, Greek historians and philosophers were beginning to criticize them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few radical philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Xenophanes" title="Xenophanes">Xenophanes</a> of Colophon were already beginning to label the poets\' tales as blasphemous lies in the 6th century&#160;BC; Xenophanes had complained that Homer and Hesiod attributed to the gods "all that is shameful and disgraceful among men; they steal, commit adultery, and deceive one another".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf169-170_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf169-170-74">[74]</a></sup> This line of thought found its most sweeping expression in <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Republic_(Plato)" title="The Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Laws_(dialogue)" title="Laws (dialogue)">Laws</a></i>. Plato created his own allegorical myths (such as the vision of Er in the <i>Republic</i>), attacked the traditional tales of the gods\' tricks, thefts and adulteries as immoral, and objected to their central role in literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup> Plato\'s criticism was the first serious challenge to the Homeric mythological tradition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanson37_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hanson37-72">[72]</a></sup> referring to the myths as "old wives\' chatter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The176b_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-The176b-75">[75]</a></sup> For his part Aristotle criticized the Pre-socratic quasi-mythical philosophical approach and underscored that "Hesiod and the theological writers were concerned only with what seemed plausible to themselves, and had no respect for us ... But it is not worth taking seriously writers who show off in the mythical style; as for those who do proceed by proving their assertions, we must cross-examine them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffin80_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin80-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nevertheless, even Plato did not manage to wean himself and his society from the influence of myth; his own characterization for <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> is based on the traditional Homeric and tragic patterns, used by the philosopher to praise the righteous life of his teacher:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Apology28b-d_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Apology28b-d-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But perhaps someone might say: "Are you then not ashamed, Socrates, of having followed such a pursuit, that you are now in danger of being put to death as a result?" But I should make to him a just reply: "You do not speak well, Sir, if you think a man in whom there is even a little merit ought to consider danger of life or death, and not rather regard this only, when he does things, whether the things he does are right or wrong and the acts of a good or a bad man. For according to your argument all the demigods would be bad who died at Troy, including the son of <a href="/wiki/Thetis" title="Thetis">Thetis</a>, who so despised danger, in comparison with enduring any disgrace, that when his mother (and she was a goddess) said to him, as he was eager to slay <a href="/wiki/Hector" title="Hector">Hector</a>, something like this, I believe,\n</p><dl><dd>My son, if you avenge the death of your friend <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a> and kill Hector, you yourself shall die; for straightway, after Hector, is death appointed unto you. (Hom. Il. 18.96)</dd></dl>\n<p>he, when he heard this, made light of death and danger, and feared much more to live as a coward and not to avenge his friends, and said,\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Straightway may I die, after doing vengeance upon the wrongdoer, that I may not stay here, jeered at beside the curved ships, a burden of the earth.</dd></dl>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Hanson and Heath estimate that Plato\'s rejection of the Homeric tradition was not favorably received by the grassroots Greek civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanson37_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hanson37-72">[72]</a></sup> The old myths were kept alive in local cults; they continued to influence poetry and to form the main subject of painting and sculpture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griffin80_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Griffin80-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More sportingly, the 5th century&#160;BC <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedian</a> Euripides often played with the old traditions, mocking them, and through the voice of his characters injecting notes of doubt. Yet the subjects of his plays were taken, without exception, from myth. Many of these plays were written in answer to a predecessor\'s version of the same or similar myth. Euripides mainly impugns the myths about the gods and begins his critique with an objection similar to the one previously expressed by <a href="/wiki/Xenocrates" title="Xenocrates">Xenocrates</a>: the gods, as traditionally represented, are far too crassly <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf169-170_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graf169-170-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_and_Roman_rationalism">Hellenistic and Roman rationalism</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CiceroBust.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="453" data-file-width="314" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/CiceroBust.jpg/170px-CiceroBust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/CiceroBust.jpg/255px-CiceroBust.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/CiceroBust.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CiceroBust.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> saw himself as the defender of the established order, despite his personal skepticism with regard to myth and his inclination towards more philosophical conceptions of divinity.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, mythology took on the prestige of elite knowledge that marks its possessors as belonging to a certain class. At the same time, the skeptical turn of the Classical age became even more pronounced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale89_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale89-77">[77]</a></sup> Greek mythographer <a href="/wiki/Euhemerus" title="Euhemerus">Euhemerus</a> established the tradition of seeking an actual historical basis for mythical beings and events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrEuh_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrEuh-78">[78]</a></sup> Although his original work (<i>Sacred Scriptures</i>) is lost, much is known about it from what is recorded by Diodorus and <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rationalizing <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a> of myth became even more popular under the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, thanks to the physicalist theories of <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicurean</a> philosophy. Stoics presented explanations of the gods and heroes as physical phenomena, while the Euhemerists rationalized them as historical figures. At the same time, the Stoics and the <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonists</a> promoted the moral significations of the mythological tradition, often based on Greek etymologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chance69_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chance69-80">[80]</a></sup> Through his Epicurean message, <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a> had sought to expel superstitious fears from the minds of his fellow-citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walshxxvi_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walshxxvi-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a>, too, is skeptical about the mythological tradition and claims that he does not intend to pass judgement on such legends (fabulae).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale88_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale88-82">[82]</a></sup> The challenge for Romans with a strong and apologetic sense of <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">religious tradition</a> was to defend that tradition while conceding that it was often a breeding-ground for superstition. The antiquarian <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Terentius_Varro" title="Marcus Terentius Varro">Varro</a>, who regarded religion as a human institution with great importance for the preservation of good in society, devoted rigorous study to the origins of religious cults. In his <i>Antiquitates Rerum Divinarum</i> (which has not survived, but <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a>\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_God_(book)" title="City of God (book)">City of God</a></i> indicates its general approach) Varro argues that whereas the superstitious man fears the gods, the truly religious person venerates them as parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walshxxvi_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walshxxvi-81">[81]</a></sup> According to Varro, there have been three accounts of deities in the Roman society: the mythical account created by poets for theatre and entertainment, the civil account used by people for veneration as well as by the city, and the natural account created by the philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barfield2011p76_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barfield2011p76-83">[83]</a></sup> The best state is, adds Varro, where the civil theology combines the poetic mythical account with the philosopher\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barfield2011p76_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barfield2011p76-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Roman Academic Cotta ridicules both literal and allegorical acceptance of myth, declaring roundly that myths have no place in philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale87_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale87-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> is also generally disdainful of myth, but, like Varro, he is emphatic in his support for the state religion and its institutions. It is difficult to know how far down the social scale this rationalism extended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale88_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gale88-82">[82]</a></sup> Cicero asserts that no one (not even old women and boys) is so foolish as to believe in the terrors of Hades or the existence of <a href="/wiki/Scylla" title="Scylla">Scyllas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centaur" title="Centaur">centaurs</a> or other composite creatures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CiceroTusc_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-CiceroTusc-85">[85]</a></sup> but, on the other hand, the orator elsewhere complains of the superstitious and credulous character of the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CiceroDiv_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-CiceroDiv-86">[86]</a></sup> <i>De Natura Deorum</i> is the most comprehensive summary of Cicero\'s line of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walshxxvii_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walshxxvii-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syncretizing_trends">Syncretizing trends</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2544" data-file-width="1084" decoding="async" height="399" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg/170px-Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg/255px-Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg/340px-Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lycian_Apollo_Louvre_left.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo (early Imperial Roman copy of a fourth-century Greek original, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> Museum).</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Roman</a> times, a new Roman mythology was born through syncretization of numerous Greek and other foreign gods. This occurred because the Romans had little <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">mythology</a> of their own, and inheritance of the Greek mythological tradition caused the major Roman gods to adopt characteristics of their Greek equivalents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale88_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gale88-82">[82]</a></sup> The gods <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> are an example of this mythological overlap. In addition to the combination of the two mythological traditions, the association of the Romans with eastern religions led to further syncretizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> For instance, the cult of Sun was introduced in Rome after <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a>\'s successful campaigns in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>. The Asiatic divinities <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mithraic_Mysteries" title="Mithraic Mysteries">Mithras</a> (that is to say, the Sun) and Ba\'al were combined with Apollo and Helios into one <a href="/wiki/Sol_Invictus" title="Sol Invictus">Sol Invictus</a>, with conglomerated rites and compound attributes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Apollo might be increasingly identified in religion with Helios or even Dionysus, but texts retelling his myths seldom reflected such developments. The traditional literary mythology was increasingly dissociated from actual religious practice. The worship of Sol as special protector of the emperors and of the empire remained the chief imperial religion until it was replaced by Christianity.\n</p><p>The surviving 2nd-century collection of <a href="/wiki/Orphism_(religion)" title="Orphism (religion)">Orphic Hymns</a> (second century AD) and the <i>Saturnalia</i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrobius_Ambrosius_Theodosius" title="Macrobius Ambrosius Theodosius">Macrobius Ambrosius Theodosius</a> (fifth century) are influenced by the theories of rationalism and the syncretizing trends as well. The Orphic Hymns are a set of pre-classical poetic compositions, attributed to Orpheus, himself the subject of a renowned myth. In reality, these poems were probably composed by several different poets, and contain a rich set of clues about prehistoric European mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The stated purpose of the <i>Saturnalia</i> is to transmit the Hellenic culture Macrobius has derived from his reading, even though much of his treatment of gods is colored by Egyptian and North African mythology and theology (which also affect the interpretation of Virgil). In Saturnalia reappear mythographical comments influenced by the Euhemerists, the Stoics and the Neoplatonists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chance69_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chance69-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_interpretations">Modern interpretations</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Modern_understanding_of_Greek_mythology" title="Modern understanding of Greek mythology">Modern understanding of Greek mythology</a></div>\n<p>The genesis of modern understanding of Greek mythology is regarded by some scholars as a double reaction at the end of the eighteenth century against "the traditional attitude of Christian animosity", in which the Christian reinterpretation of myth as a "lie" or <a href="/wiki/Fable" title="Fable">fable</a> had been retained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In Germany, by about 1795, there was a growing interest in Homer and Greek mythology. In <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen" title="G&#246;ttingen">G&#246;ttingen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Matthias_Gesner" title="Johann Matthias Gesner">Johann Matthias Gesner</a> began to revive Greek studies, while his successor, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Heyne" title="Christian Gottlob Heyne">Christian Gottlob Heyne</a>, worked with <a href="/wiki/Johann_Joachim_Winckelmann" title="Johann Joachim Winckelmann">Johann Joachim Winckelmann</a>, and laid the foundations for mythological research both in Germany and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf9_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf9-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_and_psychoanalytic_approaches">Comparative and psychoanalytic approaches</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Max_Muller.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="874" data-file-width="684" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Max_Muller.jpg/170px-Max_Muller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Max_Muller.jpg/255px-Max_Muller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Max_Muller.jpg/340px-Max_Muller.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Max_Muller.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max M&#252;ller">Max M&#252;ller</a> is regarded as one of the founders of comparative mythology. In his <i>Comparative Mythology</i> (1867) M&#252;ller analysed the "disturbing" similarity between the mythologies of "savage races" with those of the early Europeans.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comparative_mythology" title="Comparative mythology">Comparative mythology</a></div>\n<p>The development of comparative philology in the 19th century, together with ethnological discoveries in the 20th century, established the science of myth. Since the Romantics, all study of myth has been comparative. <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Mannhardt" title="Wilhelm Mannhardt">Wilhelm Mannhardt</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_George_Frazer" title="James George Frazer">James Frazer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stith_Thompson" title="Stith Thompson">Stith Thompson</a> employed the comparative approach to collect and classify the themes of folklore and mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brmyth_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brmyth-93">[93]</a></sup> In 1871 <a href="/wiki/Edward_Burnett_Tylor" title="Edward Burnett Tylor">Edward Burnett Tylor</a> published his <i>Primitive Culture</i>, in which he applied the comparative method and tried to explain the origin and evolution of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllenSegal_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllenSegal-94">[94]</a></sup> Tylor\'s procedure of drawing together material culture, ritual and myth of widely separated cultures influenced both <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Campbell" title="Joseph Campbell">Joseph Campbell</a>. <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max M&#252;ller">Max M&#252;ller</a> applied the new science of comparative mythology to the study of myth, in which he detected the distorted remains of <a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">Aryan</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">nature worship</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82aw_Malinowski" title="Bronis&#322;aw Malinowski">Bronis&#322;aw Malinowski</a> emphasized the ways myth fulfills common social functions. <a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude L&#233;vi-Strauss">Claude L&#233;vi-Strauss</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">structuralists</a> have compared the formal relations and patterns in myths throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brmyth_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brmyth-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> introduced a transhistorical and biological conception of man and a view of myth as an expression of repressed ideas. Dream interpretation is the basis of Freudian myth interpretation and Freud\'s concept of dreamwork recognizes the importance of contextual relationships for the interpretation of any individual element in a dream. This suggestion would find an important point of rapprochment between the structuralist and psychoanalytic approaches to myth in Freud\'s thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> extended the transhistorical, psychological approach with his theory of the "collective unconscious" and the archetypes (inherited "archaic" patterns), often encoded in myth, that arise out of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup> According to Jung, "myth-forming structural elements must be present in the unconscious psyche".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Comparing Jung\'s methodology with <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Campbell" title="Joseph Campbell">Joseph Campbell</a>\'s theory, Robert A. Segal concludes that "to interpret a myth Campbell simply identifies the archetypes in it. An interpretation of the <i>Odyssey</i>, for example, would show how Odysseus\'s life conforms to a heroic pattern. Jung, by contrast, considers the identification of archetypes merely the first step in the interpretation of a myth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Segal_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Segal-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/K%C3%A1roly_Ker%C3%A9nyi" title="K&#225;roly Ker&#233;nyi">Karl Ker&#233;nyi</a>, one of the founders of modern studies in Greek mythology, gave up his early views of myth, in order to apply Jung\'s theories of archetypes to Greek myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf38_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf38-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_theories">Origin theories</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_religion" title="Mycenaean religion">Mycenaean religion</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_deities" title="Mycenaean deities">Mycenaean deities</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Similarities_between_Roman,_Greek_and_Etruscan_mythologies" title="Similarities between Roman, Greek and Etruscan mythologies">Similarities between Roman, Greek and Etruscan mythologies</a></div>\n<p>Max M&#252;ller attempted to understand an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Indo-European</a> religious form by tracing it back to its Indo-European (or, in M&#252;ller\'s time, "<a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">Aryan</a>") "original" manifestation. In 1891, he claimed that "the most important discovery which has been made during the nineteenth century with respect to the ancient history of mankind ... was this sample equation: <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyaus_Pita" title="Dyaus Pita">Dyaus-pitar</a> = Greek Zeus = Latin <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> = Old Norse <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr" title="T&#253;r">Tyr</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen12_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen12-99">[99]</a></sup> The question of Greek mythology\'s place in <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies" title="Indo-European studies">Indo-European studies</a> has generated much scholarship since M&#252;ller\'s time. For example, philologist <a href="/wiki/Georges_Dum%C3%A9zil" title="Georges Dum&#233;zil">Georges Dum&#233;zil</a> draws a comparison between the Greek <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a> and the Sanskrit <a href="/wiki/Varuna" title="Varuna">Varuna</a>, although there is no hint that he believes them to be originally connected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poleman_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poleman-100">[100]</a></sup> In other cases, close parallels in character and function suggest a common heritage, yet lack of linguistic evidence makes it difficult to prove, as in the case of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Moirai" title="Moirai">Moirai</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Norns" title="Norns">Norns</a> of <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It appears that the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_religion" title="Mycenaean religion">Mycenaean religion</a> was the mother of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Greek religion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and its pantheon already included many divinities that can be found in classical Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> However, Greek mythology is generally seen as having heavy influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Greek" title="Pre-Greek">Pre-Greek</a> and Near Eastern cultures, and as such contains few important elements for the reconstruction of the Proto-Indo-European religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Consequently, Greek mythology received minimal scholarly attention in the context of Indo-European <a href="/wiki/Comparative_mythology" title="Comparative mythology">comparative mythology</a> until the mid 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Archaeology and mythography have revealed influence from Asia Minor and the Near East. <a href="/wiki/Adonis" title="Adonis">Adonis</a> seems to be the Greek counterpart&#8212;more clearly in cult than in myth&#8212;of a Near Eastern "dying god". <a href="/wiki/Cybele" title="Cybele">Cybele</a> is rooted in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> culture while much of Aphrodite\'s <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> may spring from Semitic goddesses. There are also possible parallels between the earliest divine generations (Chaos and its children) and <a href="/wiki/Tiamat" title="Tiamat">Tiamat</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/En%C3%BBma_Eli%C5%A1" title="En&#251;ma Eli&#353;">Enuma Elish</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SegaEdmunds_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-SegaEdmunds-106">[106]</a></sup> According to Meyer Reinhold, "near Eastern theogonic concepts, involving divine succession through violence and generational conflicts for power, found their way ... into Greek mythology".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to Indo-European and Near Eastern origins, some scholars have speculated on the debts of Greek mythology to the indigenous pre-Greek societies: <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, Mycenae, <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orchomenus_(Boeotia)" title="Orchomenus (Boeotia)">Orchomenus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert23_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert23-108">[108]</a></sup> Historians of religion were fascinated by a number of apparently ancient configurations of myth connected with Crete (the god as bull, Zeus and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasipha%C3%AB" title="Pasipha&#235;">Pasipha&#235;</a> who yields to the bull and gives birth to the <a href="/wiki/Minotaur" title="Minotaur">Minotaur</a>, etc.). Martin P. Nilsson asserts, based on the representations and general function of the gods, that a lot of <a href="/wiki/Minoan_religion" title="Minoan religion">Minoan gods and religious conceptions</a> were fused in the Mycenaean religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and concluded that all great classical Greek myths were tied to Mycenaean centres and anchored in prehistoric times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Nevertheless, according to Burkert, the iconography of the Cretan Palace Period has provided almost no confirmation for these theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert24_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert24-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Motifs_in_Western_art_and_literature">Motifs in Western art and literature</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology_in_western_art_and_literature" title="Greek mythology in western art and literature">Greek mythology in western art and literature</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_films_based_on_Greco-Roman_mythology" title="List of films based on Greco-Roman mythology">List of films based on Greco-Roman mythology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology_in_popular_culture" title="Greek mythology in popular culture">Greek mythology in popular culture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="18840" data-file-width="30000" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/220px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/330px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/440px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Botticelli\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_Venus_(Botticelli)" title="The Birth of Venus (Botticelli)">The Birth of Venus</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1485&#8211;1486</span>, oil on canvas, <a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>)&#8212;a revived <i>Venus Pudica</i> for a new view of pagan <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Antiquity</a>&#8212;is often said to epitomize for modern viewers the spirit of the Renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The widespread adoption of Christianity did not curb the popularity of the myths. With the rediscovery of classical antiquity in the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, the poetry of Ovid became a major influence on the imagination of poets, dramatists, musicians and artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrBurn_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrBurn-112">[112]</a></sup> From the early years of Renaissance, artists such as <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, portrayed the <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">Pagan</a> subjects of Greek mythology alongside more conventional Christian themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrBurn_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrBurn-112">[112]</a></sup> Through the medium of Latin and the works of Ovid, Greek myth influenced medieval and Renaissance poets such as <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Boccaccio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a> in Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2703" data-file-width="2285" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Draper_-_The_Lament_for_Icarus_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Lament_for_Icarus" title="The Lament for Icarus">The Lament for Icarus</a></i> (1898) by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_James_Draper" title="Herbert James Draper">Herbert James Draper</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In Northern Europe, Greek mythology never took the same hold of the visual arts, but its effect was very obvious on literature. The English imagination was fired by Greek mythology starting with <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Chaucer</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a> and continuing through <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bridges" title="Robert Bridges">Robert Bridges</a> in the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Jean_Racine" title="Jean Racine">Racine</a> in France and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a> in Germany revived Greek drama, reworking the ancient myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrBurn_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrBurn-112">[112]</a></sup> Although during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> of the 18th century reaction against Greek myth spread throughout Europe, the myths continued to provide an important source of raw material for dramatists, including those who wrote the <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">libretti</a> for many of <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a>\'s and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a>\'s operas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn75_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burn75-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> initiated a surge of enthusiasm for all things Greek, including Greek mythology. In Britain, new translations of Greek tragedies and Homer inspired contemporary poets (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Tennyson,_1st_Baron_Tennyson" title="Alfred Tennyson, 1st Baron Tennyson">Alfred Lord Tennyson</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">Keats</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Gordon_Byron,_6th_Baron_Byron" title="George Gordon Byron, 6th Baron Byron">Byron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Shelley</a>) and painters (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederic_Leighton,_1st_Baron_Leighton" title="Frederic Leighton, 1st Baron Leighton">Lord Leighton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Alma-Tadema" title="Lawrence Alma-Tadema">Lawrence Alma-Tadema</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn75-76_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burn75-76-114">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Willibald_Gluck" title="Christoph Willibald Gluck">Christoph Gluck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Offenbach" title="Jacques Offenbach">Jacques Offenbach</a> and many others set Greek mythological themes to music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup> American authors of the 19th century, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bulfinch" title="Thomas Bulfinch">Thomas Bulfinch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" title="Nathaniel Hawthorne">Nathaniel Hawthorne</a>, held that the study of the classical myths was essential to the understanding of English and American literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klatt_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klatt-115">[115]</a></sup> In more recent times, classical themes have been reinterpreted by dramatists <a href="/wiki/Jean_Anouilh" title="Jean Anouilh">Jean Anouilh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Cocteau" title="Jean Cocteau">Jean Cocteau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Giraudoux" title="Jean Giraudoux">Jean Giraudoux</a> in France, <a href="/wiki/Eugene_O%27Neill" title="Eugene O\'Neill">Eugene O\'Neill</a> in America, and <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a> in Britain and by novelists such as <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Gide" title="Andr&#233; Gide">Andr&#233; Gide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div Cartledge, <i>The Spartans</i>, 60, and <i>The Greeks</i>, 22</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Titan/Pasiphae.html" rel="nofollow">Pasiphae</a>, Encyclopedia: Greek Gods, Spirits, Monsters</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-HomerIliad366-369-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-HomerIliad366-369_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Homer, <i>Iliad</i>, 8. An epic poem about the Battle of Troy. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.is/20120526194909/http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/cgi-bin//ptext?doc=Perseus:text:1999.01.0134&amp;layout=&amp;loc=8.366" rel="nofollow">366&#8211;369</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cuthbertson, <i>Political Myth and Epic</i> (Michigan State university Press) 1975 has selected a wider range of epic, from <a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a> to Voltaire\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Henriade" title="Henriade">Henriade</a></i>, but his central theme, that myths encode mechanisms of cultural dynamics, structuring a community by creating a moral consensus, is a familiar mainstream view that applies to Greek myth.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Johnson17-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Johnson17_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Albala-Johnson-Johnson, <i>Understanding the Odyssey</i>, 17</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Johnson18-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Johnson18_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Albala-Johnson-Johnson, <i>Understanding the Odyssey</i>, 18</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Gallimach109-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gallimach109_17-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A.
